Page","templateId":"{9F5E0978-58FB-40A6-8872-3E84B897AF67}","url":"/research/zeus","searchPageUrl":"/research","fields":{"metaTitle":{"value":"ZeuS Banking Trojan Report"},"noFollow":{"value":false},"excludeFromSearch":{"value":false},"noIndex":{"value":false},"gated":{"value":false},"gatedVideoType":{"id":"971f4491-d4a6-42bf-abc8-9d305797ca0c","url":"/sitecore/content/shared/lookups/video-platforms/vimeo","name":"Vimeo","displayName":"Vimeo","fields":{"value":{"value":"vimeo"}}},"eyebrow":{"value":"Threat Analysis"},"image":{"value":{"src":"/-/media/images/insights/abstracts/0024-info-tab5/abstract_0024_infotabs5_360x190.jpg?h=190\u0026iar=0\u0026w=360\u0026hash=49BCE8CC5E7F16C9F27A90D821B16E7C","alt":"ZeuS Banking Trojan Report","width":"360","height":"190"}},"title":{"value":"ZeuS Banking Trojan Report"},"body":{"value":"\u003cul\u003e\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAuthor: \u003c/strong\u003eKevin Stevens and Don Jackson, Security Researchers\u003cbr\u003e\n\t\tSecureWorks Counter Threat Unit \u003csup\u003eSM\u003c/sup\u003e (CTU)\u003c/li\u003e\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDate: \u003c/strong\u003eMarch 10, 2010\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eIntroduction \u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis Threat Analysis from the SecureWorks CTU\u003csup\u003eSM\u003c/sup\u003e provides a brief overview of the current version of ZeuS and its modules, along with the market pricing. We will then see how ZeuS is actively being used and the irony of how the criminals themselves can sometimes be the victims.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS is a well-known banking Trojan horse program, also known as crimeware. This trojan steals data from infected computers via web browsers and protected storage. Once infected, the computer sends the stolen data to a bot command and control (C\u0026amp;C) server, where the data is stored.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS is sold in the criminal underground as a kit for around $3000-4000, and is likely the one malware most utilized by criminals specializing in financial fraud. ZeuS has evolved over time and includes a full arsenal of information stealing capabilities:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals data submitted in HTTP forms\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals account credentials stored in the Windows Protected Storage\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals client-side X.509 public key infrastructure (PKI) certificates\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals FTP and POP account credentials\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals/deletes HTTP and Flash cookies\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eModifies the HTML pages of target websites for information stealing purposes\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eRedirects victims from target web pages to attacker controlled ones\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eTakes screenshots and scrapes HTML from target sites\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSearches for and uploads files from the infected computer\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eModifies the local hosts file (%systemroot%\\system32\\drivers\\etc\\hosts)\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eDownloads and executes arbitrary programs\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eDeletes crucial registry keys, rendering the computer unable to boot into Windows\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eZeus Author Protects Code with Hardware-Based Licensing System\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe latest version of ZeuS as of this date is 1.3.4.x and is privately sold. The author has gone to great lengths to protect this version using a Hardware-based Licensing System. The author of Zeus has created a hardware-based licensing system for the Zeus Builder kit that you can only run on one computer. Once you run it, you get a code from the specific computer, and then the author gives you a key just for that computer. This is the first time we have seen this level of control for malware.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eZeus Versions\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe version number breakdown is as follows (from the ZeuS manual):\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhat do the numbers [signify] in the version?\u003cbr\u003e\na.b.c.d\u003cbr\u003e\na(1) - a complete change in the bot. This has never changed from 1.\u003cbr\u003e\nb(3) - Major changes that cause complete or partial incompatibility with the previous\u003cbr\u003e\nversions. Recently we moved from version 2 to version 3.\u003cbr\u003e\nc(2) - This is for bug fixes, improvements, and adding features.\u003cbr\u003e\nd(1) - This for a small revision in the code to make the malware undetectable by AV vendors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe latest public version the CTU has encountered is 1.2.7.19. This version is actively being traded and has the Firefox form grabber module enabled. The Firefox module allows the ZeuS trojan to grab data out of any forms completed on the Firefox web browser. The webinjects text file, which allows for the injection of fields into the Internet Explorer (IE) web browser, does not work with Firefox and only works with IE. The function of the webinjects text file is to display an extra field for a victim to complete when they log onto a banking site (see Figures 1 and 2). The extra field asks for data in addition to the username and password. This technique is similar to phishing, but the extra field is not part of the original site.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe current list of modules that work with ZeuS are as follows:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eZeus Kit for Version 1.3.4.x ($3,000 to $4,000)\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The Private Version of the Zeus Kit is running between $3,000 and $4,000. The latest private version of ZeuS, as of this date, is 1.3.4.x. This private version seems to be only sold by the author, and he is protecting all of its functionality through the hardware l I.D., locking which we mentioned previously. \u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBackconnect $1500\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The backconnect module allows an attacker to 'connect back' to the infected computer and make financial transactions from it. This way, banks that try to track where money transfers originate will always trace it back to the computer of the account holder.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFirefox form grabber $2000\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The Firefox form grabber module grabs data out of fields that are submitted using the Firefox web browser. This data can include personally identifiable information (PII) as well as usernames and passwords for bank accounts, trading accounts, online payment accounts, and anything else that would require the use of a username and password.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eJabber (IM) chat notifier $500\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The Jabber module allows an attacker to receive stolen data in \"real time\". If a bank account is being protected with a token that generates random numbers, then the attacker can access the victim's account in real time after the victim logs in using the token. An example of what would be sent via the Jabber module is:\u003cbr\u003e\n    \u003cblockquote\u003e\n    \u003cp\u003eRequest Type :Domestic Wire \u003cbr\u003e\n    Name :John Smith\u003cbr\u003e\n    Address :1234 Main Street\u003cbr\u003e\n    City :Atlanta GA 12345\u003cbr\u003e\n    Payee Name :Some Bank\u003cbr\u003e\n    Memo :Credit to acc:1111111111\u003cbr\u003e\n    \u003cbr\u003e\n    Beneficiary Account :Checking #0000001234\u003cbr\u003e\n    Beneficiary Address 1 :Georgia\u003cbr\u003e\n    Payee Bank ID :0123456\u003cbr\u003e\n    Bank Name :Some Bank \u003cbr\u003e\n    Addr1 :Atlanta Some Bank \u003cbr\u003e\n    Amount :1500000.00\u003cbr\u003e\n    From Account :My Money Market #123456789\u003cbr\u003e\n    Date Posted :01/01/10\u003cbr\u003e\n    Time Posted :2:00 PM\u003c/p\u003e\n    \u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003ch3\u003eVNC (Virtual Network Computing) private module $10,000\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe VNC module is similar to the backconnect module, except that it allows you to establish a fully functioning virtual connection. The attacker can take control of the infected computer without the victim being aware of it. Essentially, the VNC provides the hacker with not just a Network Proxy but with a Total Presence Proxy (it is the total package), allowing the hacker to use all of the victim's hardware and software, including its browser, so as to avoid a bank's fraud detection systems. Essentially, it allows the hacker to get around many hardware-based authentication systems. Additionally, if the victim is doing large dollar transactions and is required to insert a smartcard into their computer that the bank will recognize, the hacker will have access to that smartcard via the VNC module.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eWindows 7/Vista Support $2000\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis module allows the ZeuS trojan to infect these Windows 7 and Vista systems. Without it, the botnet controller is limited to Windows XP systems.\u003c/p\u003e\n\n\u003ch2\u003eZeuS 1.4 Adds Polymorphic Encryption and Web Injects for Firefox\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe authors of ZeuS are currently developing 1.4 which is being beta tested. It includes two key components which make the ZeuS Banking Trojan even more stealthy and comprehensive, due to its ability to also do web injects for the Firefox browser. The components are:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWeb Injects for Firefox\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePolymorphic Encryption:\u003c/strong\u003e The 1.4 version of ZeuS will enable the ZeuS Trojan to re-encrypt itself each time it infects a victim, thus making each infection unique. The 1.4 version also enables the ZeuS file names to be randomly generated, thus each infection will contain different file names. This will make it very difficult for anti-virus engines to identify the ZeuS Banking Trojan on the victims' system. \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003ch2\u003eHow Zeus Works\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS performs stolen data exfiltration and remote commands via encrypted HTTP POST requests to a Command and Control web server. The encryption ZeuS uses is RC4, with a key that is embedded in the binary. While the primary function of this malware is to commit financial fraud, its general information stealing behaviors make it a threat to all enterprises. Basic credential theft is not targeted, meaning the ZeuS botnet controller does not run the botnet to just see what it can get. The botnet controller usually has a financial target in mind. The criminals typically search for data of interest to directly derive value or to sell the data to another criminal.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSeveral of these tasks can be done \"on-demand\" via the HTTP-based control panel, and directed to selected infected computers. These on-demand tasks may be performed via special scripts that can be executed on select systems. These scripts can be used to take screenshots of infected systems or to do ZeuS binary updates.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"340\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_login.jpg?h=340\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=029C30F1B79D0C74C89D444B72E7220E\" style=\"height: 340px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 1. View of a login page for a financial site.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"365\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_login_two.jpg?h=365\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=20B3565E5A6FB6FFBDA32DFD16F6E115\" style=\"height: 365px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 2. View of the same page from a computer infected with ZeuS. Note it includes three new fields.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS includes these capabilities to assist with automated clearing house (ACH) fraud. ACH is an electronic network for financial transactions in the United States. It is used for online bill payments, payroll direct deposits from employers and to transfer money from one account to another. ZeuS is aimed at taking advantage of ACH to transfer money to criminal accounts.\u003c/p\u003e\n\n\u003ch3\u003eHow to detect the ZeuS Banking Trojan on your computer\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eComputers infected with this version of ZeuS will have the following files and folders installed. The location depends on whether the victim has Administrator rights. The files will most likely have the HIDDEN attribute set to hide them from casual inspection.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWith Administrator rights:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e%systemroot%\\system32\\sdra64.exe (malware) \u003cbr\u003e\n%systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\u003cbr\u003e\n%systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\\user.ds (encrypted stolen data file) %systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\\user.ds.lll (temporary file for stolen data) %systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\\local.ds (encrypted configuration file)\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eWithout Administrator rights:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e%appdata%\\sdra64.exe\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\\user.ds\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\\user.ds.lll\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\\local.ds\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS also makes registry changes to ensure that it starts up with Administrator privileges:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHKLM\\Software\\Microsoft\\Windows NT\\CurrentVersion\\Winlogon\u003cbr\u003e\nFrom:\u003cbr\u003e\n\"Userinit\" = \"C:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\userinit.exe\"\u003cbr\u003e\nTo:\u003cbr\u003e\n\"Userinit\" = \"C:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\userinit.exe,C:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\sdra64.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eWithout Administrator rights:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHKCU\\Software\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Run\u003cbr\u003e\nAdd:\u003cbr\u003e\n\"Userinit\" = \"C:\\Documents and Settings\\\u0026amp;lt;user\u0026amp;gt;\\Application Data\\sdra64.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eThe sdra64.exe program uses process injection to hide its presence in the list of running processes. Upon startup, it will inject code into winlogon.exe (if Administrator rights available) or explorer.exe (for non-Administrators) and exit. The injected code infects other processes to perform its data theft capabilities.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eTypes of data being targeted by ZeuS\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe following figures illustrate what type of data is stolen and collected from infected computers. The list of infected systems shown in Figure 3 can range from the hundreds to thousands. The number of infected systems usually depends on how long the botnet is active and how well the bot controller spreads the malware.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"326\" width=\"401\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_02.jpg?h=326\u0026amp;w=401\u0026amp;hash=6709B2A611D17A8634C6EC3B73838611\" style=\"height: 326px; width: 401px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 3. List of infected systems. Each system is a folder on the C\u0026amp;C server.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"223\" width=\"575\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_04.gif?h=223\u0026amp;w=575\u0026amp;hash=0EB1ADE2A1ED871AF40B2D712C46AA37\" style=\"height: 223px; width: 575px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 4. Sample of collected information from an infected system for a social networking site.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 4 illustrates a fragment of information collected from an infected system.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eThis listing begins with the bot id, which is usually the name of the affected computer.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eNext is the name of the botnet that includes the affected computer. Also listed is the bot version.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eNext appears the IPv4 IP address of the affected computer and the country it belongs to. This information is not always accurate because a host with an internal IP will not display correctly.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eFurther down the list appears the OS.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eThis log was captured from an Internet Explorer web browser used to visit a popular social networking website.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eZeuS captured the language used, full page parameters, email address, password, and anything else that was being generated from the login attempt.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"236\" width=\"572\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_06.gif?h=236\u0026amp;w=572\u0026amp;hash=A1D4E400E8E8E15F4499644EC5928532\" style=\"height: 236px; width: 572px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 5. Sample of banking information collected from an infected system.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 5 is similar in function to the previous listing, but this time ZeuS is stealing the login information for a bank account. Figure 6 shows the same user transferring money from checking to savings. These examples demonstrate the risks that infected computers and their users are susceptible to.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"375\" width=\"573\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_08.gif?h=375\u0026amp;w=573\u0026amp;hash=7BD36DE4AFD713A9EFD5DDD4AC4640C1\" style=\"height: 375px; width: 573px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 6. Sample of banking transaction information collected from an infected system.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"98\" width=\"623\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_10.jpg?h=98\u0026amp;w=623\u0026amp;hash=44697EB11577FF51896E791106021861\" style=\"height: 98px; width: 623px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 7. PFX (PKCS #12) Digital Certificate file collected from an infected system.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS even steals PFX (PKCS #12) Digital Certificates that the victim's web browser uses to authenticate to a site (Figure 7). So the victim is still vulnerable if their banking site uses assigned certificates.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"255\" width=\"623\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_12.jpg?h=255\u0026amp;w=623\u0026amp;hash=128D9DB66E3BFB3CE9BE4DE1B4F6012A\" style=\"height: 255px; width: 623px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 8. ZeuS server control panel, operating system statistics.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 8 shows a ZeuS server using the 1.2.4.2 version control panel. The screen displays totals of the bots and what OS version they are running, the most popular being Windows XP Professional SP3. This ZeuS bot also infected Vista users, which means the bot controller paid extra for the Vista/Windows 7 module.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"455\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_14.jpg?h=455\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=B306EF3A1BC90B58B97FD0202CCB14EA\" style=\"height: 455px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 9. ZeuS server control panel, statistics summary.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 9 shows the same botnet, listing bots by country. It lists the most prevalent country as BG (Bulgaria), but according to reports from bot operators, this value is not accurate because ZeuS could not determine the country listing from internal IP addresses. Note that this botnet has 1,827 infected computers (bots) in it and has logged 551,492 reports.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eStolen ZeuS data cache\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNow we will take a look at how botnet data gets stolen and leaked. Figure 10 shows a botnet controller looking for a partner for his ZeuS botnet.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"103\" width=\"623\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_16.jpg?h=103\u0026amp;w=623\u0026amp;hash=7EF33DCD8F58480AE37DC17DE1AEF602\" style=\"height: 103px; width: 623px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 10. Message from botnet controller looking for business partner.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNow his ZeuS server gets hacked and the database is posted online. The download contains the entire database plus the server-side ZeuS PHP files. The attacker is even happy enough to tell you what server he got it from.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"515\" width=\"627\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_18.jpg?h=515\u0026amp;w=627\u0026amp;hash=0E5C2A3BE7B6FC6B21EF700E2CEA683C\" style=\"height: 515px; width: 627px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 11. Message advertising hacked ZeuS server content.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eData Stolen by ZeuS \u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe total size of the PHP files for the ZeuS server is 20MB, and the stolen database is 900MB. The database contained login credentials from 1,827 victims located in the US, UK, Canada, Europe, etc. These login credentials were for banking, stock trading, credit union, online payment, insurance, social networking, government, and military accounts. The bank account, credit union, stock trading, and online payment credentials were for many large and medium sized institutions in the US, UK and Canada.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"565\" width=\"443\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_19.jpg?h=565\u0026amp;w=443\u0026amp;hash=B9C1769E4CB5CD280B035FFB604D8EE7\" style=\"height: 565px; width: 443px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 12. Message listing account credentials from the ZeuS database.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 12 shows the same user posting account usernames and passwords to try to prove that there is valuable data in the stolen ZeuS database file.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"456\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_21.jpg?h=456\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=97F59E752F77A6A3000B61E98A44F21B\" style=\"height: 456px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 13. Message explaining how to import the ZeuS database in MySQL.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe same user then gives details on how to import the database into a MySQL server so that anyone can start sorting through the data. He also gives the username and password for the login. Figure 13 mentions the same person that was looking for a partner in Figure 10. The CTU has observed other ZeuS databases for sale on various underground black markets. Their size is typically over 10GB, which is a botnet of approximately 23,000 infected computers (bots).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eHow to Protect Yourself from ZeuS\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe CTU recommends that businesses and home users carry out online banking and financial transactions on isolated workstations that are not used for general Internet activities, such as web browsing and reading email which could increase the risk of infection.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBusinesses may even consider using an alternative operating system for workstations accessing sensitive or financial accounts. Keep your antivirus, operating system and software patches up to date. Also do not open suspicious e-mail attachments or links from people that you do not know and even if you do know them, check with them to find out if they sent you something prior to opening the email. Additionally, awareness for both customers and employees is crucial. In particular, employees who interface with clients should be made aware of these types of threats to help triage potential on publish","dynamicPlaceholderId":"10"}},"placeholders":{"cta-actions":[]}},{"uid":"ce1d1c96-3fe4-4402-ba91-97250096cf84","componentName":"RichTextContent","dataSource":"/sitecore/content/SW/swxp-rendering/Content/Articles/research/2010/zeus","params":{"CacheClearingBehavior":"Clear on publish"},"fields":{"id":"c0b00741-68e0-44fc-857e-28f10bf59581","name":"zeus","displayName":"zeus","templateName":"Article Page","templateId":"{9F5E0978-58FB-40A6-8872-3E84B897AF67}","url":"/research/zeus","searchPageUrl":"/research","fields":{"metaTitle":{"value":"ZeuS Banking Trojan Report"},"noFollow":{"value":false},"excludeFromSearch":{"value":false},"noIndex":{"value":false},"gated":{"value":false},"gatedVideoType":{"id":"971f4491-d4a6-42bf-abc8-9d305797ca0c","url":"/sitecore/content/shared/lookups/video-platforms/vimeo","name":"Vimeo","displayName":"Vimeo","fields":{"value":{"value":"vimeo"}}},"eyebrow":{"value":"Threat Analysis"},"image":{"value":{"src":"/-/media/images/insights/abstracts/0024-info-tab5/abstract_0024_infotabs5_360x190.jpg?h=190\u0026iar=0\u0026w=360\u0026hash=49BCE8CC5E7F16C9F27A90D821B16E7C","alt":"ZeuS Banking Trojan Report","width":"360","height":"190"}},"title":{"value":"ZeuS Banking Trojan Report"},"body":{"value":"\u003cul\u003e\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAuthor: \u003c/strong\u003eKevin Stevens and Don Jackson, Security Researchers\u003cbr\u003e\n\t\tSecureWorks Counter Threat Unit \u003csup\u003eSM\u003c/sup\u003e (CTU)\u003c/li\u003e\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDate: \u003c/strong\u003eMarch 10, 2010\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eIntroduction \u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis Threat Analysis from the SecureWorks CTU\u003csup\u003eSM\u003c/sup\u003e provides a brief overview of the current version of ZeuS and its modules, along with the market pricing. We will then see how ZeuS is actively being used and the irony of how the criminals themselves can sometimes be the victims.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS is a well-known banking Trojan horse program, also known as crimeware. This trojan steals data from infected computers via web browsers and protected storage. Once infected, the computer sends the stolen data to a bot command and control (C\u0026amp;C) server, where the data is stored.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS is sold in the criminal underground as a kit for around $3000-4000, and is likely the one malware most utilized by criminals specializing in financial fraud. ZeuS has evolved over time and includes a full arsenal of information stealing capabilities:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals data submitted in HTTP forms\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals account credentials stored in the Windows Protected Storage\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals client-side X.509 public key infrastructure (PKI) certificates\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals FTP and POP account credentials\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals/deletes HTTP and Flash cookies\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eModifies the HTML pages of target websites for information stealing purposes\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eRedirects victims from target web pages to attacker controlled ones\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eTakes screenshots and scrapes HTML from target sites\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSearches for and uploads files from the infected computer\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eModifies the local hosts file (%systemroot%\\system32\\drivers\\etc\\hosts)\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eDownloads and executes arbitrary programs\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eDeletes crucial registry keys, rendering the computer unable to boot into Windows\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eZeus Author Protects Code with Hardware-Based Licensing System\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe latest version of ZeuS as of this date is 1.3.4.x and is privately sold. The author has gone to great lengths to protect this version using a Hardware-based Licensing System. The author of Zeus has created a hardware-based licensing system for the Zeus Builder kit that you can only run on one computer. Once you run it, you get a code from the specific computer, and then the author gives you a key just for that computer. This is the first time we have seen this level of control for malware.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eZeus Versions\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe version number breakdown is as follows (from the ZeuS manual):\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhat do the numbers [signify] in the version?\u003cbr\u003e\na.b.c.d\u003cbr\u003e\na(1) - a complete change in the bot. This has never changed from 1.\u003cbr\u003e\nb(3) - Major changes that cause complete or partial incompatibility with the previous\u003cbr\u003e\nversions. Recently we moved from version 2 to version 3.\u003cbr\u003e\nc(2) - This is for bug fixes, improvements, and adding features.\u003cbr\u003e\nd(1) - This for a small revision in the code to make the malware undetectable by AV vendors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe latest public version the CTU has encountered is 1.2.7.19. This version is actively being traded and has the Firefox form grabber module enabled. The Firefox module allows the ZeuS trojan to grab data out of any forms completed on the Firefox web browser. The webinjects text file, which allows for the injection of fields into the Internet Explorer (IE) web browser, does not work with Firefox and only works with IE. The function of the webinjects text file is to display an extra field for a victim to complete when they log onto a banking site (see Figures 1 and 2). The extra field asks for data in addition to the username and password. This technique is similar to phishing, but the extra field is not part of the original site.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe current list of modules that work with ZeuS are as follows:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eZeus Kit for Version 1.3.4.x ($3,000 to $4,000)\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The Private Version of the Zeus Kit is running between $3,000 and $4,000. The latest private version of ZeuS, as of this date, is 1.3.4.x. This private version seems to be only sold by the author, and he is protecting all of its functionality through the hardware l I.D., locking which we mentioned previously. \u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBackconnect $1500\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The backconnect module allows an attacker to 'connect back' to the infected computer and make financial transactions from it. This way, banks that try to track where money transfers originate will always trace it back to the computer of the account holder.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFirefox form grabber $2000\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The Firefox form grabber module grabs data out of fields that are submitted using the Firefox web browser. This data can include personally identifiable information (PII) as well as usernames and passwords for bank accounts, trading accounts, online payment accounts, and anything else that would require the use of a username and password.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eJabber (IM) chat notifier $500\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The Jabber module allows an attacker to receive stolen data in \"real time\". If a bank account is being protected with a token that generates random numbers, then the attacker can access the victim's account in real time after the victim logs in using the token. An example of what would be sent via the Jabber module is:\u003cbr\u003e\n    \u003cblockquote\u003e\n    \u003cp\u003eRequest Type :Domestic Wire \u003cbr\u003e\n    Name :John Smith\u003cbr\u003e\n    Address :1234 Main Street\u003cbr\u003e\n    City :Atlanta GA 12345\u003cbr\u003e\n    Payee Name :Some Bank\u003cbr\u003e\n    Memo :Credit to acc:1111111111\u003cbr\u003e\n    \u003cbr\u003e\n    Beneficiary Account :Checking #0000001234\u003cbr\u003e\n    Beneficiary Address 1 :Georgia\u003cbr\u003e\n    Payee Bank ID :0123456\u003cbr\u003e\n    Bank Name :Some Bank \u003cbr\u003e\n    Addr1 :Atlanta Some Bank \u003cbr\u003e\n    Amount :1500000.00\u003cbr\u003e\n    From Account :My Money Market #123456789\u003cbr\u003e\n    Date Posted :01/01/10\u003cbr\u003e\n    Time Posted :2:00 PM\u003c/p\u003e\n    \u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003ch3\u003eVNC (Virtual Network Computing) private module $10,000\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe VNC module is similar to the backconnect module, except that it allows you to establish a fully functioning virtual connection. The attacker can take control of the infected computer without the victim being aware of it. Essentially, the VNC provides the hacker with not just a Network Proxy but with a Total Presence Proxy (it is the total package), allowing the hacker to use all of the victim's hardware and software, including its browser, so as to avoid a bank's fraud detection systems. Essentially, it allows the hacker to get around many hardware-based authentication systems. Additionally, if the victim is doing large dollar transactions and is required to insert a smartcard into their computer that the bank will recognize, the hacker will have access to that smartcard via the VNC module.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eWindows 7/Vista Support $2000\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis module allows the ZeuS trojan to infect these Windows 7 and Vista systems. Without it, the botnet controller is limited to Windows XP systems.\u003c/p\u003e\n\n\u003ch2\u003eZeuS 1.4 Adds Polymorphic Encryption and Web Injects for Firefox\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe authors of ZeuS are currently developing 1.4 which is being beta tested. It includes two key components which make the ZeuS Banking Trojan even more stealthy and comprehensive, due to its ability to also do web injects for the Firefox browser. The components are:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWeb Injects for Firefox\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePolymorphic Encryption:\u003c/strong\u003e The 1.4 version of ZeuS will enable the ZeuS Trojan to re-encrypt itself each time it infects a victim, thus making each infection unique. The 1.4 version also enables the ZeuS file names to be randomly generated, thus each infection will contain different file names. This will make it very difficult for anti-virus engines to identify the ZeuS Banking Trojan on the victims' system. \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003ch2\u003eHow Zeus Works\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS performs stolen data exfiltration and remote commands via encrypted HTTP POST requests to a Command and Control web server. The encryption ZeuS uses is RC4, with a key that is embedded in the binary. While the primary function of this malware is to commit financial fraud, its general information stealing behaviors make it a threat to all enterprises. Basic credential theft is not targeted, meaning the ZeuS botnet controller does not run the botnet to just see what it can get. The botnet controller usually has a financial target in mind. The criminals typically search for data of interest to directly derive value or to sell the data to another criminal.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSeveral of these tasks can be done \"on-demand\" via the HTTP-based control panel, and directed to selected infected computers. These on-demand tasks may be performed via special scripts that can be executed on select systems. These scripts can be used to take screenshots of infected systems or to do ZeuS binary updates.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"340\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_login.jpg?h=340\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=029C30F1B79D0C74C89D444B72E7220E\" style=\"height: 340px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 1. View of a login page for a financial site.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"365\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_login_two.jpg?h=365\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=20B3565E5A6FB6FFBDA32DFD16F6E115\" style=\"height: 365px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 2. View of the same page from a computer infected with ZeuS. Note it includes three new fields.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS includes these capabilities to assist with automated clearing house (ACH) fraud. ACH is an electronic network for financial transactions in the United States. It is used for online bill payments, payroll direct deposits from employers and to transfer money from one account to another. ZeuS is aimed at taking advantage of ACH to transfer money to criminal accounts.\u003c/p\u003e\n\n\u003ch3\u003eHow to detect the ZeuS Banking Trojan on your computer\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eComputers infected with this version of ZeuS will have the following files and folders installed. The location depends on whether the victim has Administrator rights. The files will most likely have the HIDDEN attribute set to hide them from casual inspection.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWith Administrator rights:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e%systemroot%\\system32\\sdra64.exe (malware) \u003cbr\u003e\n%systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\u003cbr\u003e\n%systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\\user.ds (encrypted stolen data file) %systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\\user.ds.lll (temporary file for stolen data) %systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\\local.ds (encrypted configuration file)\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eWithout Administrator rights:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e%appdata%\\sdra64.exe\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\\user.ds\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\\user.ds.lll\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\\local.ds\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS also makes registry changes to ensure that it starts up with Administrator privileges:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHKLM\\Software\\Microsoft\\Windows NT\\CurrentVersion\\Winlogon\u003cbr\u003e\nFrom:\u003cbr\u003e\n\"Userinit\" = \"C:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\userinit.exe\"\u003cbr\u003e\nTo:\u003cbr\u003e\n\"Userinit\" = \"C:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\userinit.exe,C:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\sdra64.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eWithout Administrator rights:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHKCU\\Software\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Run\u003cbr\u003e\nAdd:\u003cbr\u003e\n\"Userinit\" = \"C:\\Documents and Settings\\\u0026amp;lt;user\u0026amp;gt;\\Application Data\\sdra64.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eThe sdra64.exe program uses process injection to hide its presence in the list of running processes. Upon startup, it will inject code into winlogon.exe (if Administrator rights available) or explorer.exe (for non-Administrators) and exit. The injected code infects other processes to perform its data theft capabilities.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eTypes of data being targeted by ZeuS\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe following figures illustrate what type of data is stolen and collected from infected computers. The list of infected systems shown in Figure 3 can range from the hundreds to thousands. The number of infected systems usually depends on how long the botnet is active and how well the bot controller spreads the malware.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"326\" width=\"401\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_02.jpg?h=326\u0026amp;w=401\u0026amp;hash=6709B2A611D17A8634C6EC3B73838611\" style=\"height: 326px; width: 401px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 3. List of infected systems. Each system is a folder on the C\u0026amp;C server.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"223\" width=\"575\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_04.gif?h=223\u0026amp;w=575\u0026amp;hash=0EB1ADE2A1ED871AF40B2D712C46AA37\" style=\"height: 223px; width: 575px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 4. Sample of collected information from an infected system for a social networking site.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 4 illustrates a fragment of information collected from an infected system.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eThis listing begins with the bot id, which is usually the name of the affected computer.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eNext is the name of the botnet that includes the affected computer. Also listed is the bot version.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eNext appears the IPv4 IP address of the affected computer and the country it belongs to. This information is not always accurate because a host with an internal IP will not display correctly.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eFurther down the list appears the OS.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eThis log was captured from an Internet Explorer web browser used to visit a popular social networking website.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eZeuS captured the language used, full page parameters, email address, password, and anything else that was being generated from the login attempt.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"236\" width=\"572\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_06.gif?h=236\u0026amp;w=572\u0026amp;hash=A1D4E400E8E8E15F4499644EC5928532\" style=\"height: 236px; width: 572px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 5. Sample of banking information collected from an infected system.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 5 is similar in function to the previous listing, but this time ZeuS is stealing the login information for a bank account. Figure 6 shows the same user transferring money from checking to savings. These examples demonstrate the risks that infected computers and their users are susceptible to.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"375\" width=\"573\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_08.gif?h=375\u0026amp;w=573\u0026amp;hash=7BD36DE4AFD713A9EFD5DDD4AC4640C1\" style=\"height: 375px; width: 573px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 6. Sample of banking transaction information collected from an infected system.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"98\" width=\"623\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_10.jpg?h=98\u0026amp;w=623\u0026amp;hash=44697EB11577FF51896E791106021861\" style=\"height: 98px; width: 623px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 7. PFX (PKCS #12) Digital Certificate file collected from an infected system.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS even steals PFX (PKCS #12) Digital Certificates that the victim's web browser uses to authenticate to a site (Figure 7). So the victim is still vulnerable if their banking site uses assigned certificates.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"255\" width=\"623\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_12.jpg?h=255\u0026amp;w=623\u0026amp;hash=128D9DB66E3BFB3CE9BE4DE1B4F6012A\" style=\"height: 255px; width: 623px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 8. ZeuS server control panel, operating system statistics.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 8 shows a ZeuS server using the 1.2.4.2 version control panel. The screen displays totals of the bots and what OS version they are running, the most popular being Windows XP Professional SP3. This ZeuS bot also infected Vista users, which means the bot controller paid extra for the Vista/Windows 7 module.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"455\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_14.jpg?h=455\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=B306EF3A1BC90B58B97FD0202CCB14EA\" style=\"height: 455px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 9. ZeuS server control panel, statistics summary.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 9 shows the same botnet, listing bots by country. It lists the most prevalent country as BG (Bulgaria), but according to reports from bot operators, this value is not accurate because ZeuS could not determine the country listing from internal IP addresses. Note that this botnet has 1,827 infected computers (bots) in it and has logged 551,492 reports.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eStolen ZeuS data cache\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNow we will take a look at how botnet data gets stolen and leaked. Figure 10 shows a botnet controller looking for a partner for his ZeuS botnet.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"103\" width=\"623\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_16.jpg?h=103\u0026amp;w=623\u0026amp;hash=7EF33DCD8F58480AE37DC17DE1AEF602\" style=\"height: 103px; width: 623px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 10. Message from botnet controller looking for business partner.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNow his ZeuS server gets hacked and the database is posted online. The download contains the entire database plus the server-side ZeuS PHP files. The attacker is even happy enough to tell you what server he got it from.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"515\" width=\"627\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_18.jpg?h=515\u0026amp;w=627\u0026amp;hash=0E5C2A3BE7B6FC6B21EF700E2CEA683C\" style=\"height: 515px; width: 627px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 11. Message advertising hacked ZeuS server content.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eData Stolen by ZeuS \u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe total size of the PHP files for the ZeuS server is 20MB, and the stolen database is 900MB. The database contained login credentials from 1,827 victims located in the US, UK, Canada, Europe, etc. These login credentials were for banking, stock trading, credit union, online payment, insurance, social networking, government, and military accounts. The bank account, credit union, stock trading, and online payment credentials were for many large and medium sized institutions in the US, UK and Canada.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"565\" width=\"443\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_19.jpg?h=565\u0026amp;w=443\u0026amp;hash=B9C1769E4CB5CD280B035FFB604D8EE7\" style=\"height: 565px; width: 443px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 12. Message listing account credentials from the ZeuS database.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 12 shows the same user posting account usernames and passwords to try to prove that there is valuable data in the stolen ZeuS database file.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"456\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_21.jpg?h=456\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=97F59E752F77A6A3000B61E98A44F21B\" style=\"height: 456px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 13. Message explaining how to import the ZeuS database in MySQL.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe same user then gives details on how to import the database into a MySQL server so that anyone can start sorting through the data. He also gives the username and password for the login. Figure 13 mentions the same person that was looking for a partner in Figure 10. The CTU has observed other ZeuS databases for sale on various underground black markets. Their size is typically over 10GB, which is a botnet of approximately 23,000 infected computers (bots).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eHow to Protect Yourself from ZeuS\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe CTU recommends that businesses and home users carry out online banking and financial transactions on isolated workstations that are not used for general Internet activities, such as web browsing and reading email which could increase the risk of infection.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBusinesses may even consider using an alternative operating system for workstations accessing sensitive or financial accounts. Keep your antivirus, operating system and software patches up to date. Also do not open suspicious e-mail attachments or links from people that you do not know and even if you do know them, check with them to find out if they sent you something prior to opening the email. Additionally, awareness for both customers and employees is crucial. In particular, employees who interface with clients should be made aware of these types of threats to help triage potential victims.\u003c/p\u003e"},"author":[{"id":"fe3cf2f9-20a4-49ff-a7f0-be9da4225d03","url":"http://uswest-www.secureworks.com/content/people/kevin-stevens","name":"Kevin Stevens","displayName":"Kevin Destination Page Url":"\u003clink text=\"\" anchor=\"\" linktype=\"internal\" class=\"\" title=\"\" target=\"\" querystring=\"\" id=\"{9CCE9D90-CF8F-4210-B503-5474D48B4928}\" /\u003e"},"fields":{"id":"c0b00741-68e0-44fc-857e-28f10bf59581","name":"zeus","displayName":"zeus","templateName":"Article Page","templateId":"{9F5E0978-58FB-40A6-8872-3E84B897AF67}","url":"/research/zeus","searchPageUrl":"/research","fields":{"metaTitle":{"value":"ZeuS Banking Trojan Report"},"noFollow":{"value":false},"excludeFromSearch":{"value":false},"noIndex":{"value":false},"gated":{"value":false},"gatedVideoType":{"id":"971f4491-d4a6-42bf-abc8-9d305797ca0c","url":"/sitecore/content/shared/lookups/video-platforms/vimeo","name":"Vimeo","displayName":"Vimeo","fields":{"value":{"value":"vimeo"}}},"eyebrow":{"value":"Threat Analysis"},"image":{"value":{"src":"/-/media/images/insights/abstracts/0024-info-tab5/abstract_0024_infotabs5_360x190.jpg?h=190\u0026iar=0\u0026w=360\u0026hash=49BCE8CC5E7F16C9F27A90D821B16E7C","alt":"ZeuS Banking Trojan Report","width":"360","height":"190"}},"title":{"value":"ZeuS Banking Trojan Report"},"body":{"value":"\u003cul\u003e\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAuthor: \u003c/strong\u003eKevin Stevens and Don Jackson, Security Researchers\u003cbr\u003e\n\t\tSecureWorks Counter Threat Unit \u003csup\u003eSM\u003c/sup\u003e (CTU)\u003c/li\u003e\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDate: \u003c/strong\u003eMarch 10, 2010\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eIntroduction \u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis Threat Analysis from the SecureWorks CTU\u003csup\u003eSM\u003c/sup\u003e provides a brief overview of the current version of ZeuS and its modules, along with the market pricing. We will then see how ZeuS is actively being used and the irony of how the criminals themselves can sometimes be the victims.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS is a well-known banking Trojan horse program, also known as crimeware. This trojan steals data from infected computers via web browsers and protected storage. Once infected, the computer sends the stolen data to a bot command and control (C\u0026amp;C) server, where the data is stored.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS is sold in the criminal underground as a kit for around $3000-4000, and is likely the one malware most utilized by criminals specializing in financial fraud. ZeuS has evolved over time and includes a full arsenal of information stealing capabilities:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals data submitted in HTTP forms\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals account credentials stored in the Windows Protected Storage\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals client-side X.509 public key infrastructure (PKI) certificates\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals FTP and POP account credentials\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSteals/deletes HTTP and Flash cookies\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eModifies the HTML pages of target websites for information stealing purposes\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eRedirects victims from target web pages to attacker controlled ones\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eTakes screenshots and scrapes HTML from target sites\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eSearches for and uploads files from the infected computer\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eModifies the local hosts file (%systemroot%\\system32\\drivers\\etc\\hosts)\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eDownloads and executes arbitrary programs\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eDeletes crucial registry keys, rendering the computer unable to boot into Windows\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eZeus Author Protects Code with Hardware-Based Licensing System\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe latest version of ZeuS as of this date is 1.3.4.x and is privately sold. The author has gone to great lengths to protect this version using a Hardware-based Licensing System. The author of Zeus has created a hardware-based licensing system for the Zeus Builder kit that you can only run on one computer. Once you run it, you get a code from the specific computer, and then the author gives you a key just for that computer. This is the first time we have seen this level of control for malware.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eZeus Versions\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe version number breakdown is as follows (from the ZeuS manual):\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhat do the numbers [signify] in the version?\u003cbr\u003e\na.b.c.d\u003cbr\u003e\na(1) - a complete change in the bot. This has never changed from 1.\u003cbr\u003e\nb(3) - Major changes that cause complete or partial incompatibility with the previous\u003cbr\u003e\nversions. Recently we moved from version 2 to version 3.\u003cbr\u003e\nc(2) - This is for bug fixes, improvements, and adding features.\u003cbr\u003e\nd(1) - This for a small revision in the code to make the malware undetectable by AV vendors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe latest public version the CTU has encountered is 1.2.7.19. This version is actively being traded and has the Firefox form grabber module enabled. The Firefox module allows the ZeuS trojan to grab data out of any forms completed on the Firefox web browser. The webinjects text file, which allows for the injection of fields into the Internet Explorer (IE) web browser, does not work with Firefox and only works with IE. The function of the webinjects text file is to display an extra field for a victim to complete when they log onto a banking site (see Figures 1 and 2). The extra field asks for data in addition to the username and password. This technique is similar to phishing, but the extra field is not part of the original site.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe current list of modules that work with ZeuS are as follows:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eZeus Kit for Version 1.3.4.x ($3,000 to $4,000)\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The Private Version of the Zeus Kit is running between $3,000 and $4,000. The latest private version of ZeuS, as of this date, is 1.3.4.x. This private version seems to be only sold by the author, and he is protecting all of its functionality through the hardware l I.D., locking which we mentioned previously. \u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBackconnect $1500\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The backconnect module allows an attacker to 'connect back' to the infected computer and make financial transactions from it. This way, banks that try to track where money transfers originate will always trace it back to the computer of the account holder.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFirefox form grabber $2000\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The Firefox form grabber module grabs data out of fields that are submitted using the Firefox web browser. This data can include personally identifiable information (PII) as well as usernames and passwords for bank accounts, trading accounts, online payment accounts, and anything else that would require the use of a username and password.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eJabber (IM) chat notifier $500\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n    The Jabber module allows an attacker to receive stolen data in \"real time\". If a bank account is being protected with a token that generates random numbers, then the attacker can access the victim's account in real time after the victim logs in using the token. An example of what would be sent via the Jabber module is:\u003cbr\u003e\n    \u003cblockquote\u003e\n    \u003cp\u003eRequest Type :Domestic Wire \u003cbr\u003e\n    Name :John Smith\u003cbr\u003e\n    Address :1234 Main Street\u003cbr\u003e\n    City :Atlanta GA 12345\u003cbr\u003e\n    Payee Name :Some Bank\u003cbr\u003e\n    Memo :Credit to acc:1111111111\u003cbr\u003e\n    \u003cbr\u003e\n    Beneficiary Account :Checking #0000001234\u003cbr\u003e\n    Beneficiary Address 1 :Georgia\u003cbr\u003e\n    Payee Bank ID :0123456\u003cbr\u003e\n    Bank Name :Some Bank \u003cbr\u003e\n    Addr1 :Atlanta Some Bank \u003cbr\u003e\n    Amount :1500000.00\u003cbr\u003e\n    From Account :My Money Market #123456789\u003cbr\u003e\n    Date Posted :01/01/10\u003cbr\u003e\n    Time Posted :2:00 PM\u003c/p\u003e\n    \u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003ch3\u003eVNC (Virtual Network Computing) private module $10,000\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe VNC module is similar to the backconnect module, except that it allows you to establish a fully functioning virtual connection. The attacker can take control of the infected computer without the victim being aware of it. Essentially, the VNC provides the hacker with not just a Network Proxy but with a Total Presence Proxy (it is the total package), allowing the hacker to use all of the victim's hardware and software, including its browser, so as to avoid a bank's fraud detection systems. Essentially, it allows the hacker to get around many hardware-based authentication systems. Additionally, if the victim is doing large dollar transactions and is required to insert a smartcard into their computer that the bank will recognize, the hacker will have access to that smartcard via the VNC module.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eWindows 7/Vista Support $2000\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis module allows the ZeuS trojan to infect these Windows 7 and Vista systems. Without it, the botnet controller is limited to Windows XP systems.\u003c/p\u003e\n\n\u003ch2\u003eZeuS 1.4 Adds Polymorphic Encryption and Web Injects for Firefox\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe authors of ZeuS are currently developing 1.4 which is being beta tested. It includes two key components which make the ZeuS Banking Trojan even more stealthy and comprehensive, due to its ability to also do web injects for the Firefox browser. The components are:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWeb Injects for Firefox\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePolymorphic Encryption:\u003c/strong\u003e The 1.4 version of ZeuS will enable the ZeuS Trojan to re-encrypt itself each time it infects a victim, thus making each infection unique. The 1.4 version also enables the ZeuS file names to be randomly generated, thus each infection will contain different file names. This will make it very difficult for anti-virus engines to identify the ZeuS Banking Trojan on the victims' system. \u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003ch2\u003eHow Zeus Works\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS performs stolen data exfiltration and remote commands via encrypted HTTP POST requests to a Command and Control web server. The encryption ZeuS uses is RC4, with a key that is embedded in the binary. While the primary function of this malware is to commit financial fraud, its general information stealing behaviors make it a threat to all enterprises. Basic credential theft is not targeted, meaning the ZeuS botnet controller does not run the botnet to just see what it can get. The botnet controller usually has a financial target in mind. The criminals typically search for data of interest to directly derive value or to sell the data to another criminal.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSeveral of these tasks can be done \"on-demand\" via the HTTP-based control panel, and directed to selected infected computers. These on-demand tasks may be performed via special scripts that can be executed on select systems. These scripts can be used to take screenshots of infected systems or to do ZeuS binary updates.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"340\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_login.jpg?h=340\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=029C30F1B79D0C74C89D444B72E7220E\" style=\"height: 340px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 1. View of a login page for a financial site.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"365\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_login_two.jpg?h=365\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=20B3565E5A6FB6FFBDA32DFD16F6E115\" style=\"height: 365px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 2. View of the same page from a computer infected with ZeuS. Note it includes three new fields.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS includes these capabilities to assist with automated clearing house (ACH) fraud. ACH is an electronic network for financial transactions in the United States. It is used for online bill payments, payroll direct deposits from employers and to transfer money from one account to another. ZeuS is aimed at taking advantage of ACH to transfer money to criminal accounts.\u003c/p\u003e\n\n\u003ch3\u003eHow to detect the ZeuS Banking Trojan on your computer\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eComputers infected with this version of ZeuS will have the following files and folders installed. The location depends on whether the victim has Administrator rights. The files will most likely have the HIDDEN attribute set to hide them from casual inspection.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWith Administrator rights:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e%systemroot%\\system32\\sdra64.exe (malware) \u003cbr\u003e\n%systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\u003cbr\u003e\n%systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\\user.ds (encrypted stolen data file) %systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\\user.ds.lll (temporary file for stolen data) %systemroot%\\system32\\lowsec\\local.ds (encrypted configuration file)\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eWithout Administrator rights:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e%appdata%\\sdra64.exe\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\\user.ds\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\\user.ds.lll\u003cbr\u003e\n%appdata%\\lowsec\\local.ds\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS also makes registry changes to ensure that it starts up with Administrator privileges:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHKLM\\Software\\Microsoft\\Windows NT\\CurrentVersion\\Winlogon\u003cbr\u003e\nFrom:\u003cbr\u003e\n\"Userinit\" = \"C:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\userinit.exe\"\u003cbr\u003e\nTo:\u003cbr\u003e\n\"Userinit\" = \"C:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\userinit.exe,C:\\WINDOWS\\system32\\sdra64.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eWithout Administrator rights:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHKCU\\Software\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Run\u003cbr\u003e\nAdd:\u003cbr\u003e\n\"Userinit\" = \"C:\\Documents and Settings\\\u0026amp;lt;user\u0026amp;gt;\\Application Data\\sdra64.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eThe sdra64.exe program uses process injection to hide its presence in the list of running processes. Upon startup, it will inject code into winlogon.exe (if Administrator rights available) or explorer.exe (for non-Administrators) and exit. The injected code infects other processes to perform its data theft capabilities.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eTypes of data being targeted by ZeuS\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe following figures illustrate what type of data is stolen and collected from infected computers. The list of infected systems shown in Figure 3 can range from the hundreds to thousands. The number of infected systems usually depends on how long the botnet is active and how well the bot controller spreads the malware.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"326\" width=\"401\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_02.jpg?h=326\u0026amp;w=401\u0026amp;hash=6709B2A611D17A8634C6EC3B73838611\" style=\"height: 326px; width: 401px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 3. List of infected systems. Each system is a folder on the C\u0026amp;C server.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"223\" width=\"575\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_04.gif?h=223\u0026amp;w=575\u0026amp;hash=0EB1ADE2A1ED871AF40B2D712C46AA37\" style=\"height: 223px; width: 575px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 4. Sample of collected information from an infected system for a social networking site.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 4 illustrates a fragment of information collected from an infected system.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eThis listing begins with the bot id, which is usually the name of the affected computer.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eNext is the name of the botnet that includes the affected computer. Also listed is the bot version.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eNext appears the IPv4 IP address of the affected computer and the country it belongs to. This information is not always accurate because a host with an internal IP will not display correctly.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eFurther down the list appears the OS.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eThis log was captured from an Internet Explorer web browser used to visit a popular social networking website.\u003c/li\u003e\n    \u003cli\u003eZeuS captured the language used, full page parameters, email address, password, and anything else that was being generated from the login attempt.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"236\" width=\"572\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_06.gif?h=236\u0026amp;w=572\u0026amp;hash=A1D4E400E8E8E15F4499644EC5928532\" style=\"height: 236px; width: 572px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 5. Sample of banking information collected from an infected system.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 5 is similar in function to the previous listing, but this time ZeuS is stealing the login information for a bank account. Figure 6 shows the same user transferring money from checking to savings. These examples demonstrate the risks that infected computers and their users are susceptible to.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"375\" width=\"573\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_08.gif?h=375\u0026amp;w=573\u0026amp;hash=7BD36DE4AFD713A9EFD5DDD4AC4640C1\" style=\"height: 375px; width: 573px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 6. Sample of banking transaction information collected from an infected system.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"98\" width=\"623\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_10.jpg?h=98\u0026amp;w=623\u0026amp;hash=44697EB11577FF51896E791106021861\" style=\"height: 98px; width: 623px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 7. PFX (PKCS #12) Digital Certificate file collected from an infected system.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eZeuS even steals PFX (PKCS #12) Digital Certificates that the victim's web browser uses to authenticate to a site (Figure 7). So the victim is still vulnerable if their banking site uses assigned certificates.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"255\" width=\"623\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_12.jpg?h=255\u0026amp;w=623\u0026amp;hash=128D9DB66E3BFB3CE9BE4DE1B4F6012A\" style=\"height: 255px; width: 623px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 8. ZeuS server control panel, operating system statistics.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 8 shows a ZeuS server using the 1.2.4.2 version control panel. The screen displays totals of the bots and what OS version they are running, the most popular being Windows XP Professional SP3. This ZeuS bot also infected Vista users, which means the bot controller paid extra for the Vista/Windows 7 module.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"455\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_14.jpg?h=455\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=B306EF3A1BC90B58B97FD0202CCB14EA\" style=\"height: 455px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 9. ZeuS server control panel, statistics summary.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 9 shows the same botnet, listing bots by country. It lists the most prevalent country as BG (Bulgaria), but according to reports from bot operators, this value is not accurate because ZeuS could not determine the country listing from internal IP addresses. Note that this botnet has 1,827 infected computers (bots) in it and has logged 551,492 reports.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eStolen ZeuS data cache\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNow we will take a look at how botnet data gets stolen and leaked. Figure 10 shows a botnet controller looking for a partner for his ZeuS botnet.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"103\" width=\"623\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_16.jpg?h=103\u0026amp;w=623\u0026amp;hash=7EF33DCD8F58480AE37DC17DE1AEF602\" style=\"height: 103px; width: 623px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 10. Message from botnet controller looking for business partner.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNow his ZeuS server gets hacked and the database is posted online. The download contains the entire database plus the server-side ZeuS PHP files. The attacker is even happy enough to tell you what server he got it from.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"515\" width=\"627\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_18.jpg?h=515\u0026amp;w=627\u0026amp;hash=0E5C2A3BE7B6FC6B21EF700E2CEA683C\" style=\"height: 515px; width: 627px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 11. Message advertising hacked ZeuS server content.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eData Stolen by ZeuS \u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe total size of the PHP files for the ZeuS server is 20MB, and the stolen database is 900MB. The database contained login credentials from 1,827 victims located in the US, UK, Canada, Europe, etc. These login credentials were for banking, stock trading, credit union, online payment, insurance, social networking, government, and military accounts. The bank account, credit union, stock trading, and online payment credentials were for many large and medium sized institutions in the US, UK and Canada.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"565\" width=\"443\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_19.jpg?h=565\u0026amp;w=443\u0026amp;hash=B9C1769E4CB5CD280B035FFB604D8EE7\" style=\"height: 565px; width: 443px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 12. Message listing account credentials from the ZeuS database.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 12 shows the same user posting account usernames and passwords to try to prove that there is valuable data in the stolen ZeuS database file.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"456\" width=\"624\" src=\"/-/media/images/insights/resources/threat-analysis/044-zeus/zeus_trojan_21.jpg?h=456\u0026amp;w=624\u0026amp;hash=97F59E752F77A6A3000B61E98A44F21B\" style=\"height: 456px; width: 624px;\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\n\u003cem\u003eFigure 13. Message explaining how to import the ZeuS database in MySQL.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe same user then gives details on how to import the database into a MySQL server so that anyone can start sorting through the data. He also gives the username and password for the login. Figure 13 mentions the same person that was looking for a partner in Figure 10. The CTU has observed other ZeuS databases for sale on various underground black markets. Their size is typically over 10GB, which is a botnet of approximately 23,000 infected computers (bots).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eHow to Protect Yourself from ZeuS\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe CTU recommends that businesses and home users carry out online banking and financial transactions on isolated workstations that are not used for general Internet activities, such as web browsing and reading email which could increase the risk of infection.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBusinesses may even consider using an alternative operating system for workstations accessing sensitive or financial accounts. Keep your antivirus, operating system and software patches up to date. Also do not open suspicious e-mail attachments or links from people that you do not know and even if you do know them, check with them to find out if they sent you something prior to opening the email. Additionally, awareness for both customers and employees is crucial. In particular, employees who interface with clients should be made aware of these types of threats to help triage potential victims.\u003c/p\u003e"},"author":[{"id":"fe3cf2f9-20a4-49ff-a7f0-be9da4225d03","url":"http://uswest-www.secureworks.com/content/people/kevin-stevens","name":"Kevin Stevens","displayName":"Kevin /\u003e"}}},{"uid":"706aa1e8-81f9-491f-a471-710ad8670d33","componentName":"AboutAuthor","dataSource":"/sitecore/content/SW/swxp-rendering/Content/Articles/research/2010/zeus","fields":{"id":"c0b00741-68e0-44fc-857e-28f10bf59581","name":"zeus","displayName":"zeus","templateName":"Article Page","templateId":"{9F5E0978-58FB-40A6-8872-3E84B897AF67}","url":"/research/zeus","searchPageUrl":"/research","fields":{"metaTitle":{"value":"ZeuS Banking Trojan
